                    }
                }

<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="A streamlined and predominantly diploid genome in the tiny marine green alga Chloropicon primus"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2019 10:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-09-06"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Tiny marine green algae issued from two deep branches of the Chlorophyta, the Mamiellophyceae and Chloropicophyceae, dominate different regions of the oceans and play key roles in planktonic communities. Considering that the Mamiellophyceae is the sole lineage of prasinophyte algae that has been intensively investigated, the extent to which these two algal groups differ in their metabolic capacities and cellular processes is currently unknown. To address this gap of knowledge, we investigate here the nuclear genome sequence of a member of the Chloropicophyceae, Chloropicon primus. Among the main biological insights that emerge from this 17.4&#8201;Mb genome, we find an unexpected diploid structure for most chromosomes and a propionate detoxification pathway in green algae. Our results support the notion that separate events of genome minimization, which entailed differential losses of genes/pathways, have occurred in the Chloropicophyceae and Mamiellophyceae, suggesting different strategies of adaptation to oceanic environments. The Chloropicophyceae represent an important group of green algae in tropical oceans, but there is only limited genomic resource available. Here, the authors present the genome sequence of Chloropicon primus, revealing a diploid structure and the presence of a propionate detoxification pathway."/>

                    <p class="c-article-info-details" data-container-section="info">
                        
    <a data-test="journal-link" href="/ncomms"><i data-test="journal-title">Nature Communications</i></a>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Tiny marine green algae issued from two deep branches of the Chlorophyta, the Mamiellophyceae and Chloropicophyceae, dominate different regions of the oceans and play key roles in planktonic communities. Considering that the Mamiellophyceae is the sole lineage of prasinophyte algae that has been intensively investigated, the extent to which these two algal groups differ in their metabolic capacities and cellular processes is currently unknown. To address this gap of knowledge, we investigate here the nuclear genome sequence of a member of the Chloropicophyceae, <i>Chloropicon primus</i>. Among the main biological insights that emerge from this 17.4 Mb genome, we find an unexpected diploid structure for most chromosomes and a propionate detoxification pathway in green algae. Our results support the notion that separate events of genome minimization, which entailed differential losses of genes/pathways, have occurred in the Chloropicophyceae and Mamiellophyceae, suggesting different strategies of adaptation to oceanic environments.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Green algae are morphologically, ecologically, and phylogenetically diverse, and represent one of the most successful groups of photosynthetic eukaryotes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Leliaert, F. et al. Phylogeny and molecular evolution of the green algae. Crit. Rev. Plant Sci. 31, 1–46 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d42345e410">1</a></sup>; yet our knowledge about the genetic factors determining their ecological success is still limited. Soon after their emergence about 1000 Mya, early green algae split into two phyla: Chlorophyta, comprising the vast majority of extant species, and Streptophyta, comprising charophyte algae and all land plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Leliaert, F. et al. Phylogeny and molecular evolution of the green algae. Crit. Rev. Plant Sci. 31, 1–46 (2012)." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d42345e414">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Leliaert, F. et al. Chloroplast phylogenomic analyses reveal the deepest-branching lineage of the Chlorophyta, Palmophyllophyceae class. nov. Sci. Rep. 6, 25367 (2016)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d42345e414_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Lemieux, C., Otis, C. &amp; Turmel, M. A clade uniting the green algae Mesostigma viride and Chlorokybus atmophyticus represents the deepest branch of the Streptophyta in chloroplast genome-based phylogenies. BMC Biol. 5, 2 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d42345e417">3</a></sup>. In recent years, the genomes of a number of photosynthetic green algae, mainly from the Chlorophyta, have been sequenced to unravel the evolutionary trajectories followed by ancestral green algae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Derelle, E. et al. Genome analysis of the smallest free-living eukaryote Ostreococcus tauri unveils many unique features. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 103, 11647–11652 (2006)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d42345e421">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Moreau, H. et al. Gene functionalities and genome structure in Bathycoccus prasinos reflect cellular specializations at the base of the green lineage. Genome Biol. 13, R74 (2012)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d42345e421_1">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Worden, A. Z. et al. Green evolution and dynamic adaptations revealed by genomes of the marine picoeukaryotes Micromonas. Science 324, 268–272 (2009)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d42345e421_2">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Palenik, B. et al. The tiny eukaryote Ostreococcus provides genomic insights into the paradox of plankton speciation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 104, 7705–7710 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d42345e424">7</a></sup>, decipher cellular mechanisms such as the basis of multicellularity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hanschen, E. R. et al. The Gonium pectorale genome demonstrates co-option of cell cycle regulation during the evolution of multicellularity. Nat. Commun. 7, 11370 (2016)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d42345e428">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Merchant, S. S. et al. The Chlamydomonas genome reveals the evolution of key animal and plant functions. Science 318, 245–250 (2007)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d42345e428_1">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Prochnik, S. E. et al. Genomic analysis of organismal complexity in the multicellular green alga Volvox carteri. Science 329, 223–226 (2010)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d42345e428_2">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Featherston, J. et al. The 4-Celled Tetrabaena socialis nuclear genome reveals the essential components for genetic control of cell number at the origin of multicellularity in the volvocine lineage. Mol. Biol. Evol. 35, 855–870 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d42345e431">11</a></sup>, pinpoint genes linked to adaptations to ecological niches<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Blanc, G. et al. The genome of the polar eukaryotic microalga Coccomyxa subellipsoidea reveals traits of cold adaptation. Genome Biol. 13, R39 (2012)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d42345e435">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Blanc, G. et al. The Chlorella variabilis NC64A genome reveals adaptation to photosymbiosis, coevolution with viruses, and cryptic sex. Plant Cell 22, 2943–2955 (2010)." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d42345e435_1">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hirooka, S. et al. Acidophilic green algal genome provides insights into adaptation to an acidic environment. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 114, E8304–E8313 (2017)." href="#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d42345e435_2">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Hori, K. et al. Klebsormidium flaccidum genome reveals primary factors for plant terrestrial adaptation. Nat. Commun. 5, 3978 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d42345e438">15</a></sup>, and investigate metabolic networks, including routes for the biosynthesis of highly valuable compounds for the industry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bogen, C. et al. Reconstruction of the lipid metabolism for the microalga Monoraphidium neglectum from its genome sequence reveals characteristics suitable for biofuel production. BMC Genomics 14, 926 (2013)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d42345e443">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Roth, M. S. et al. Chromosome-level genome assembly and transcriptome of the green alga Chromochloris zofingiensis illuminates astaxanthin production. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 114, E4296–E4305 (2017)." href="#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d42345e443_1">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Arriola, M. B. et al. Genome sequences of Chlorella sorokiniana UTEX 1602 and Micractinium conductrix SAG 241.80: implications to maltose excretion by a green alga. Plant J. 93, 566–586 (2018)." href="#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d42345e443_2">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Hovde, B. T. et al. Genomic characterization reveals significant divergence within Chlorella sorokiniana (Chlorellales, Trebouxiophyceae). Algal Res. 35, 449–461 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d42345e446">19</a></sup>.</p><p>Known for their important role in the global carbon cycle, the green algae traditionally considered as prasinophytes constitute a paraphyletic assemblage of unicellular, predominantly marine organisms at the base of the Chlorophyta<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Leliaert, F. et al. Chloroplast phylogenomic analyses reveal the deepest-branching lineage of the Chlorophyta, Palmophyllophyceae class. nov. Sci. Rep. 6, 25367 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d42345e453">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Lemieux, C., Otis, C. &amp; Turmel, M. Six newly sequenced chloroplast genomes from prasinophyte green algae provide insights into the relationships among prasinophyte lineages and the diversity of streamlined genome architecture in picoplanktonic species. BMC Genomics 15, 857 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d42345e456">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Sym, S. D. In Marine Protists: Diversity and Dynamics (eds Ohtsuka, S. et al.) (Springer, Japan, 2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d42345e459">21</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#Fig1">1a</a>). Decoding their genomes is thus expected to yield important insights into the nature of the first green algae and their patterns of diversification. It has been suggested that the earliest-diverging green algae were scaly flagellates that were able to capture bacteria, although only a few extant prasinophytes such as <i>Cymbomonas</i> (Pyramimonadophyceae) exhibit a phagotrophic lifestyle<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Leliaert, F. et al. Phylogeny and molecular evolution of the green algae. Crit. Rev. Plant Sci. 31, 1–46 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d42345e469">1</a></sup>. Among the independent prasinophyte lineages that have been identified so far, at least five (Prasinococcales, Mamiellophyceae, Pycnococcaceae, Picocystophyceae, and Chloropicophyceae) comprise coccoid (no flagella, no scales) species of small size (≤5 μm in diameter). A highly reduced, coccoid growth form is thought to confer a distinct advantage to planktonic algae because the resulting higher surface area to volume ratio enhances the efficiency of nutrient uptake, thus increasing competitiveness in an environment poor in nutrients; furthermore, a reduced size helps to escape predators and promotes buoyancy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sym, S. D. In Marine Protists: Diversity and Dynamics (eds Ohtsuka, S. et al.) (Springer, Japan, 2015)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d42345e473">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Grimsley, N., Yau, S., Piganeau, G. &amp; Moreau, H. In Marine Protists: Diversity and Dynamics (eds Ohtsuka, S., Suzaki, T., Horiguchi, T., Suzuki, N. &amp; Not, F.) (Springer, Japan, 2015)." href="#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d42345e473_1">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Potter, D., Lajeunesse, T. C., Saunders, G. W. &amp; Anderson, R. A. Convergent evolution masks extensive biodiversity among marine coccoid picoplankton. Biodivers. Conserv. 6, 99–107 (1997)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d42345e476">23</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-12014-x/MediaObjects/41467_2019_12014_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-12014-x/MediaObjects/41467_2019_12014_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="626" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Phylogenetic position and genome features of <i>Chloropicon</i> relative to the 12 green algae selected for comparative analyses. <b>a</b> Schematic diagram depicting the main lineages of the Chloroplastida. The algae of our study group belong to the highlighted lineages. <b>b</b> Nuclear phylogenomic tree inferred from 792 concatenated proteins encoded by single-copy genes and comparison of genome sizes, numbers of chromosomes/DNA assemblies, and sizes of protein-coding gene repertoires. <b>c</b> Venn diagram showing the numbers of protein-coding genes that <i>Chloropicon</i> shares with the Mamiellophyceae, core chlorophytes and <i>Klebsormidium</i>. <b>d</b> Density of SNPs and small indels in coding (blue bars) and non-coding (intron, orange bars; intergenic sequences, gray bars) regions of individual <i>Chloropicon</i> chromosomes. The source data of <b>d</b> are provided as a Source Data file</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The Mamiellophyceae comprises the smallest known free-living eukaryote, the coccoid <i>Ostreococcus tauri</i>, and is the only prasinophyte lineage for which complete nuclear genomes are currently available (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#Fig1">1b</a>). The tiny algae belonging to the three genera from the order Mamiellales—<i>Ostreococcus</i>, <i>Micromonas</i> (no scales and one flagellum), and <i>Bathycoccus</i> (scales but no flagella)—are typically found in coastal waters although they have been observed in open oceanic waters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Sym, S. D. In Marine Protists: Diversity and Dynamics (eds Ohtsuka, S. et al.) (Springer, Japan, 2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d42345e546">21</a></sup>. For each of these genera, cryptic species derived from separate clades or strains representing different ecotypes were targeted for genome analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Derelle, E. et al. Genome analysis of the smallest free-living eukaryote Ostreococcus tauri unveils many unique features. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 103, 11647–11652 (2006)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d42345e550">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Moreau, H. et al. Gene functionalities and genome structure in Bathycoccus prasinos reflect cellular specializations at the base of the green lineage. Genome Biol. 13, R74 (2012)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d42345e550_1">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Worden, A. Z. et al. Green evolution and dynamic adaptations revealed by genomes of the marine picoeukaryotes Micromonas. Science 324, 268–272 (2009)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d42345e550_2">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Palenik, B. et al. The tiny eukaryote Ostreococcus provides genomic insights into the paradox of plankton speciation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 104, 7705–7710 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d42345e553">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="van Baren, M. J. et al. Evidence-based green algal genomics reveals marine diversity and ancestral characteristics of land plants. BMC Genomics 17, 267 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d42345e556">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Vannier, T. et al. Survey of the green picoalga Bathycoccus genomes in the global ocean. Sci. Rep. 6, 37900 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d42345e559">25</a></sup>. All examined mamiellophyceans possess reduced genomes (12–22 Mb with only ~8000–11,000 predicted genes, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#Fig1">1b</a>) compared to freshwater/terrestrial green algae and the prasinophyte <i>Cymbomonas tetramitiformis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Burns, J. A., Paasch, A., Narechania, A. &amp; Kim, E. Comparative genomics of a bacterivorous green alga reveals evolutionary causalities and consequences of phago-mixotrophic mode of nutrition. Genome Biol. Evol. 7, 3047–3061 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d42345e568">26</a></sup>, implying that genome minimization occurred along cell reduction and simplification in the course of prasinophyte evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Grimsley, N., Yau, S., Piganeau, G. &amp; Moreau, H. In Marine Protists: Diversity and Dynamics (eds Ohtsuka, S., Suzaki, T., Horiguchi, T., Suzuki, N. &amp; Not, F.) (Springer, Japan, 2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d42345e572">22</a></sup>. The <i>Ostreococcus</i> genus exhibits the smallest genomes among sequenced green algae and as in other unicellular green algae, each chromosome is present in one copy. Within each genus of the Mamiellophyceae, species divergence or adaptation to ecological niches was linked to dynamic genome evolution, with dispensable and fast-evolving genes predominantly located in the smallest of the two outlier chromosomes displaying lower GC content. The small outlier chromosome is thought to be specialized in defense against viruses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Yau, S. et al. A viral immunity chromosome in the marine picoeukaryote, Ostreococcus tauri. PLoS Pathog. 12, e1005965 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d42345e580">27</a></sup>, while the larger appears to contain the sex-determining region (SDR)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Derelle, E. et al. Genome analysis of the smallest free-living eukaryote Ostreococcus tauri unveils many unique features. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 103, 11647–11652 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d42345e584">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Worden, A. Z. et al. Green evolution and dynamic adaptations revealed by genomes of the marine picoeukaryotes Micromonas. Science 324, 268–272 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d42345e587">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Blanc-Mathieu, R. et al. Population genomics of picophytoplankton unveils novel chromosome hypervariability. Sci. Adv. 3, e1700239 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d42345e590">28</a></sup>.</p><p>Recently, a late-diverging lineage of pico/nano-prasinophytes, named Chloropicophyceae, has emerged as playing a key role in marine phytoplankton communities, especially in moderately oligotrophic waters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Lopes Dos Santos, A. et al. Diversity and oceanic distribution of prasinophytes clade VII, the dominant group of green algae in oceanic waters. ISME J. 11, 512–528 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d42345e597">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Lopes Dos Santos, A. et al. Chloropicophyceae, a new class of picophytoplanktonic prasinophytes. Sci. Rep. 7, 14019 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d42345e600">30</a></sup>. This genetically diversified lineage, which includes two genera (<i>Chloropicon</i> and <i>Chloroparvula</i>), occupies a sister position relative to the core Chlorophyta<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Leliaert, F. et al. Chloroplast phylogenomic analyses reveal the deepest-branching lineage of the Chlorophyta, Palmophyllophyceae class. nov. Sci. Rep. 6, 25367 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d42345e610">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Lemieux, C., Otis, C. &amp; Turmel, M. Six newly sequenced chloroplast genomes from prasinophyte green algae provide insights into the relationships among prasinophyte lineages and the diversity of streamlined genome architecture in picoplanktonic species. BMC Genomics 15, 857 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d42345e613">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Lopes Dos Santos, A. et al. Chloropicophyceae, a new class of picophytoplanktonic prasinophytes. Sci. Rep. 7, 14019 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d42345e616">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Turmel, M., Lopes Dos Santos, A., Otis, C., Sergerie, R. &amp; Lemieux, C. Tracing the evolution of the plastome and mitogenome in the Chloropicophyceae uncovered convergent tRNA gene losses and a variant plastid genetic code. Genome Biol. Evol. 11, 1275–1292 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d42345e619">31</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#Fig1">1a</a>) and although its members represent the dominant green algal group in open oceanic waters of tropical regions, little is known about their life cycle (sexual reproduction has not been observed) and their biochemical and physiological features.</p><p>In the present study, we report the nuclear genome sequence of <i>Chloropicon primus</i>, a member of the Chloropicophyceae. Our analyses of this 17 Mb genome provide important insights into the biology of this picoalga and the extent to which the Chloropicophyceae and Mamiellophyceae differ in their metabolic capacities and cellular processes. Major features distinguishing <i>Chloropicon</i> from most other sequenced green algae include a predominantly diploid structure, with one of the 20 chromosomes present in three copies, and a metabolic pathway for degradation of propionate.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">The <i>Chloropicon</i> nuclear genome is small and compact</h3><p>The <i>Chloropicon</i> nuclear genome was assembled into 20 chromosomes, all of which were sequenced from telomere to telomere, with CCTAAAAA as the canonical sequence of the telomeric unit. Sizes of the individual chromosomes vary from 0.37 to 1.88 Mb, for a total genome size of 17,400,691 bp, an intermediate value between the <i>Bathycoccus</i> and <i>Micromonas</i> genomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#Fig1">1b</a>). The <i>Chloropicon</i> genome is dense in coding sequences, with averages of 0.50 gene per kb and 0.50 predicted intron (average size of 152 bp) per gene (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM4">1</a>), and the repeats identified in non-coding regions represent only 4% of the genome. No major difference in overall GC content was noted across chromosomes; however, short regions of a few chromosomes—in particular, the regions containing polyketide synthase genes in chromosomes 3, 5, and 8—are characterized by a lower GC content (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#Fig2">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-12014-x/MediaObjects/41467_2019_12014_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-12014-x/MediaObjects/41467_2019_12014_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="682" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Physical map of the <i>Chloropicon</i> genome. Center. Scanning transmission electron microscopic image of this alga (provided by W. Eikrem and T. Pollina, University of Oslo). Outer ring. The 20 chromosomes were labeled from I to XX (largest to smallest; magenta to purple). Inner rings 2–10. Distribution of <i>Chloropicon</i> genes across the KEGG metabolic pathways. From outside to inside; (2) genetic information processing, (3) environmental information processing, (4) cellular processes, (5) carbohydrate metabolism, (6) energy metabolism, (7) lipid metabolism, (8) amino acid, and nucleotide metabolism, (9) glycan biosynthesis and metabolism, (10) cofactors, vitamins, terpenoids, and polyketides metabolism. Inner ring 11. Illumina (paired-end + mate pair) sequencing depth below/above average (370×) is colored in blue/light blue (<i>y</i>-axis min-max: 100–700). Inner ring 12. PacBio sequencing depth below/above average (73×) is colored in red/light red (<i>y</i>-axis min-max: 0–150). Inner ring 13. GC content of <i>Chloropicon</i> chromosomes. Background is colored in purple/light purple for values below/above average (57.5 %) (<i>y</i>-axis min-max: 37.5–77.5). Regions displaying GC content lower than 48% or higher than 70% are highlighted underneath by gold and gray lines, respectively. Inner rings 14–26. Genes shared between <i>Chloropicon</i> and other green algal genomes; (14) <i>K. flaccidum</i> (gold), (15–19) <i>O. tauri</i>, <i>O. lucimarinus</i>, <i>M. pusilla</i>, <i>M. commoda</i> and <i>B. prasinos</i> (green), [20–23] <i>Helicosporidium</i> sp., <i>C. subellipsoidea</i>, <i>C. variabilis</i> and <i>A. protothecoides</i> (blue), [24–26] <i>G. pectorale</i>, <i>C. reinhardtii</i> and <i>V. carteri</i> (magenta)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>A set of 8639 protein-coding genes were predicted in the <i>Chloropicon</i> genome based on the curated sum of evidence built from gene prediction, mapping of cDNAs from normalized libraries, and homology searches (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM5">2</a>). Of these genes, 7294 (84.4%) were found to be expressed at an average RNAseq depth ≥1.0 (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM5">2</a>) and 4765 were assigned putative functions (cutoff <i>E</i>-value = 1.0E-10), with 3723 further assigned to KEGG orthologs. Nearly 78% of the predicted <i>Chloropicon</i> proteins are shared with the 12 other green algae used in our genome comparisons (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#Fig1">1c</a>). Of the 1909 proteins unique to <i>Chloropicon</i>, only 163 featured putative functions as inferred using our annotation pipeline and GhostKOALA, and 82, 3, and 145 yielded strongest hits to proteins of bacteria, archaea, and eukaryotes from non-green lineages, respectively, in BLASTP searches (cutoff <i>E</i>-value = 1.0E-10) against the nr database of the National Center for Biotechnology Information (NCBI).</p><p>As an independent method to assess the quality and completeness of our genome assembly and annotation, we queried using BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d42345e786">32</a></sup> the annotated proteins of <i>Chloropicon</i> and 13 other green algae against the OrthoDB v10 database, which contains the collection of 2168 near-universal single-copy orthologs found in the Chlorophyta (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM1">1</a>). The <i>Chloropicon</i> proteins accounted for 82.5% of this collection, a value slightly lower than those obtained for <i>Ostreococcus tauri</i> (88.9%), <i>Bathycoccus</i> (86.9%), and <i>Gonium</i> (86.9%).</p><p>As observed for the Mamiellales, there is a limited number of gene families in <i>Chloropicon</i>, with copies of identical or very similar sequences often occurring as tandem repeats. The largest families include genes that are dispersed throughout the genome and these encode polyketide synthases (12 members, Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM6">3</a>), sialyltransferases (59 members, Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM7">4</a>), guanylyl cyclases (30 members, Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM8">5</a>), and proteins similar to transposases of the IS605 insertion sequence family (25 members, Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM9">6</a>).</p><p>In terms of gene synteny, the <i>Chloropicon</i> genome is highly divergent compared to previously sequenced green algal genomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM1">2</a>). The largest syntenic block, a suite of only 27 gene pairs, was found to be shared with the trebouxiophyte <i>Coccomyxa</i>. This observation contrasts with the high degree of gene colocalization within the chromosomes of mamiellaleans (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM1">2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Most <i>Chloropicon</i> chromosomes have a diploid structure</h3><p>Polymorphisms are pervasive in the <i>Chloropicon</i> genome, an unexpected discovery considering that all unicellular green algae analyzed so far are haploids and that the <i>Chloropicon</i> culture was derived from a clone. A total of 7282 single-nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) and 2048 small insertions/deletions (indels) were identified by aligning the Illumina reads against the genome sequence, for an average of 0.54 variants per kb (i.e. 0.05% divergence), with minimum and maximum values of 0.17 and 1.24 for chromosomes XVII and XX, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#Fig1">1d</a> and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM10">7</a>). Each polymorphic site features two alleles and two allelic combinations are readily distinguishable in aligned reads spanning two or more SNP sites. Except for a few regions, the polymorphic sites are distributed uniformly along each chromosome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#Fig3">3</a>). In coding regions which represent 73.8% of the genome, SNPs and small indels showed a 3.5- to 3.8-fold lower density than in intergenic regions and introns, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#Fig1">1d</a>). Furthermore, 73 indels larger than 100 bp were identified by mapping PacBio reads onto the chromosomal assemblies (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#Fig3">3</a> and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM11">8</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-12014-x/MediaObjects/41467_2019_12014_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-12014-x/MediaObjects/41467_2019_12014_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1031" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Variation in GC content and distribution of polymorphic loci and repeats throughout individual <i>Chloropicon</i> chromosomes. The curves shown in blue were obtained by plotting the numbers of SNPs and small indels present in sliding windows of 10,000 nt with a 5000-nt step; dispersed repeats were masked before the identification of these polymorphisms. The orange curves depict the percentages of nucleotides that are part of repeats in the same windows, while the gray curves depict the percentages of nucleotides with guanine and cytosine bases. The locations of indels ≥100 bp are denoted by arrows pointing up (deletions) and down (insertions), and the loci of putative <i>MID</i> genes by magenta diamonds. Chromosomes are labeled with Roman numbers and their lengths are represented to scale on the corresponding <i>x</i>-axis. The scale bar represents 200 kb</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>For all chromosomes, except IV and XVII, plots of the allelic frequencies observed at each polymorphic locus displayed a bell shape distribution with a mean of 50% (i.e. 1:1 allelic ratio), a result consistent with a diploid structure (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#Fig4">4a</a>). However, the distribution observed for chromosome XVII was bimodal with means of 33 and 66%, as predicted for a trisomic state (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#Fig4">4b</a>). The latter interpretation is supported by the finding that the coverage depth estimated for chromosome XVII was 1.5-fold greater relative to other chromosomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#Fig4">4d</a>). With regards to chromosome IV, the allelic frequency distribution appeared to be a composite of the two patterns just described (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#Fig4">4c</a>), and strikingly, the polymorphic loci strongly departing from the 1:1 ratio are tightly clustered in a region covering 420 kb (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#Fig5">5</a>). Note that shorter segments on other chromosomes also revealed aberrant allelic ratios (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM1">3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-12014-x/MediaObjects/41467_2019_12014_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-12014-x/MediaObjects/41467_2019_12014_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="415" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Allelic frequencies of SNPs and small indels, and coverage depth of sequencing reads on <i>Chloropicon</i> chromosomes. <b>a</b> Allelic frequency distribution observed for all chromosomes, except IV and XVII. <b>b</b> Allelic frequency distribution observed for chromosome XVII. <b>c</b> Allelic frequency distribution observed for chromosome IV. <b>d</b> Mean PacBio and Illumina (paired-end) sequencing depths of each chromosome. Source data are provided as a Source Data file</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-12014-x/MediaObjects/41467_2019_12014_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" ratio on chromosome IV. <b>a</b> Distribution of polymorphic sites with allelic frequencies falling in the following categories: 40–60% (blue) and 20–39% + 61–80% (orange). <b>b</b> Allelic frequency distribution observed in the segment delimited by positions 1–540,000. <b>c</b> Allelic frequency distribution observed in the segment delimited by positions 540,001–960,000. Source data are provided as a Source Data file</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Cellular copy number of <i>Chloropicon</i> organelle genomes</h3><p>No polymorphisms were detected by mapping the sequencing reads generated in the course of this study against the 64.3-kb plastome and 37.9-kb mitogenome reference sequences available for <i>C</i>. <i>primus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Lemieux, C., Otis, C. &amp; Turmel, M. Six newly sequenced chloroplast genomes from prasinophyte green algae provide insights into the relationships among prasinophyte lineages and the diversity of streamlined genome architecture in picoplanktonic species. BMC Genomics 15, 857 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1006">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Turmel, M., Lopes Dos Santos, A., Otis, C., Sergerie, R. &amp; Lemieux, C. Tracing the evolution of the plastome and mitogenome in the Chloropicophyceae uncovered convergent tRNA gene losses and a variant plastid genetic code. Genome Biol. Evol. 11, 1275–1292 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1009">31</a></sup>. Comparison of the coverage depth of PacBio subreads (449x and 253x for the plastome and mitogenome, respectively) to the mean PacBio sequencing depth obtained for the 19 chromosomes present in two copies (73×) enabled us to estimate that each <i>Chloropicon</i> cell, which contains a single chloroplast and a single mitochondrion, houses about 12 plastid and seven mitochondrial DNA molecules. These values are respectively 7- and 9-fold lower compared to those reported for <i>C</i>. <i>reinhardtii</i> (over 80 copies of the 205-kb plastome and about 130 copies of the 15.8-kb mitogenome as estimated by Gallaher et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Gallaher, S. D. et al. High-throughput sequencing of the chloroplast and mitochondrion of Chlamydomonas reinhardtii to generate improved de novo assemblies, analyze expression patterns and transcript speciation, and evaluate diversity among laboratory strains and wild isolates. Plant J. 93, 545–565 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1023">33</a></sup>); however, they are higher compared to the estimates provided for the Mamiellophyceae (4–6 copies for cell)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Grimsley, N., Yau, S., Piganeau, G. &amp; Moreau, H. In Marine Protists: Diversity and Dynamics (eds Ohtsuka, S., Suzaki, T., Horiguchi, T., Suzuki, N. &amp; Not, F.) (Springer, Japan, 2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1027">22</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Genes involved in meiosis and production of flagella</h3><p>As reported for most of the chlorophytes previously investigated, the meiosis-specific genes encoding SPO11–2, DCM1, Rad51c and XRCC3, MSH4, and MSH5 are present in <i>Chloropicon</i> and there is no indication that these genes are pseudogenes. We detected nil or very low levels of expression of these genes under vegetative growth (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM12">9</a>).</p><p>Although vegetative cells of <i>Chloropicon</i> lack flagella, they possess an almost full complement of protein genes known to be involved in flagellar structure and maintenance (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM13">10</a>). Other non-motile chlorophytes such as <i>Ostreococcus</i>, <i>Bathycoccus</i>, and <i>Chlorella</i> have also retained protein genes of this nature, but unlike <i>Chloropicon</i>, they are missing numerous genes involved in intraflagellar transport and radial-spoke formation. Furthermore, although no scales were distinguished on the three-layer wall of <i>Chloropicon</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Lopes Dos Santos, A. et al. Chloropicophyceae, a new class of picophytoplanktonic prasinophytes. Sci. Rep. 7, 14019 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1069">30</a></sup>, our analyses unveiled a large number of N- and O-glycan modifying enzymes that have been implicated in scale formation based on their specific occurrence and/or enrichment in the scale-bearing prasinophytes <i>Bathycoccus</i> and <i>Dolichomastix</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="van Baren, M. J. et al. Evidence-based green algal genomics reveals marine diversity and ancestral characteristics of land plants. BMC Genomics 17, 267 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1078">24</a></sup>. Notably, 59 genes encoding sialyltransferases and nine encoding sialidases were identified, the great majority of which are silent or transcribed at very low levels under the growth conditions used (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM7">4</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Search for a candidate chromosome carrying the SDR</h3><p>The primarily diploid and heterozygous structure of the <i>Chloropicon</i> genome suggests that fusion between haploid gametes of different genotypes yielded a diploid individual that reproduced subsequently by mitosis. If gametes of distinct mating types (<i>mt</i><sup><i>+</i></sup> and <i>mt</i><sup><i>−</i></sup>) were engaged in this mating as is the case for heterothallic volvocine algae, one would expect the <i>Chloropicon</i> SDR (mating-type locus) to be heterozygous in the same way as vegetative diploids of <i>Chlamydomonas</i> and to exhibit a significant difference in size and gene content between the two homologous SDR copies; moreover, the copy derived from the <i>mt</i><sup>−</sup> gamete would be expected to carry the master sex determination <i>MID</i> gene coding for a transcription factor with a DNA-binding RWP-RK domain<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Coelho, S. M., Gueno, J., Lipinska, A. P., Cock, J. M. &amp; Umen, J. G. UV chromosomes and haploid sexual systems. Trends Plant. Sci. 23, 794–807 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1123">34</a></sup>. As the cause or consequence of recombination suppression, the SDR region usually exhibits genomic rearrangements and a high density of repeated sequences. Our genome analyses revealed nine <i>Chloropicon</i> genes encoding putative RWP-RK transcription factors, but none was part of a large indel region or a region exceptionally rich in repeats or polymorphisms (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#Fig3">3</a>), and sequence alignment of motifs 1 and 2 of these proteins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Yamazaki, T. et al. Genomic structure and evolution of the mating type locus in the green seaweed Ulva partita. Sci. Rep. 7, 11679 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1134">35</a></sup> with those present in the Mid proteins of volvocine algae revealed no obvious ortholog of Mid (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM1">4</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Genes for light-harvesting complex proteins</h3><p>The chlorophyll- and carotenoid-binding proteins making up the light-harvesting complexes associated with photosystem II (LHCII) and photosystem I underwent considerable changes during the evolution of the Chloroplastida<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Ballottari, M., Girardon, J., Dall’osto, L. &amp; Bassi, R. Evolution and functional properties of photosystem II light harvesting complexes in eukaryotes. Biochim. Biophys. Acta 1817, 143–157 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1149">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Koziol, A. G. et al. Tracing the evolution of the light-harvesting antennae in chlorophyll a/b-containing organisms. Plant Physiol. 143, 1802–1816 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1152">37</a></sup>. The LHCII proteins (Lhcb) are especially diverse. Besides the invariably conserved minor Lhcb4 (CP29) and Lhcb5 (CP26) proteins, mamiellophyceans feature at least five prasinophyte-specific LHCII proteins (Lhcp)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Koziol, A. G. et al. Tracing the evolution of the light-harvesting antennae in chlorophyll a/b-containing organisms. Plant Physiol. 143, 1802–1816 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1156">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Six, C., Worden, A. Z., Rodriguez, F., Moreau, H. &amp; Partensky, F. New insights into the nature and phylogeny of prasinophyte antenna proteins: Ostreococcus tauri, a case study. Mol. Biol. Evol. 22, 2217–2230 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1159">38</a></sup>, whose evolutionary origin predates the divergence of streptophytes and chlorophytes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Koziol, A. G. et al. Tracing the evolution of the light-harvesting antennae in chlorophyll a/b-containing organisms. Plant Physiol. 143, 1802–1816 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1163">37</a></sup>. In contrast, all six <i>Chloropicon</i> Lhcb proteins are typical of core chlorophytes (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM14">11</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Carotenoid biosynthesis genes</h3><p>The Chloropicophyceae contain the basic set of pigments found in the Chlorophyceae (neoxanthin, violaxanthin, lutein, zeaxanthin, antheraxanthin, and β-β carotene) as well as loroxanthin<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Lopes Dos Santos, A. et al. Chloropicophyceae, a new class of picophytoplanktonic prasinophytes. Sci. Rep. 7, 14019 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1181">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Lopes Dos Santos, A., Gourvil, P., Rodriguez, F., Garrido, J. L. &amp; Vaulot, D. Photosynthetic pigments of oceanic Chlorophyta belonging to prasinophytes clade VII. J. Phycol. 52, 148–155 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1184">39</a></sup>. In addition, like <i>Haematococcus pluvialis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Solovchenko, A. E. Recent breakthroughs in the biology of astaxanthin accumulation by microalgal cell. Photosynth. Res. 125, 437–449 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1190">40</a></sup> and <i>Chromochloris zofingiensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Liu, J. et al. Utilization of cane molasses towards cost-saving astaxanthin production by a Chlorella zofingiensis mutant. J. Appl. Phycol. 25, 1447–1456 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1196">41</a></sup>, most members of the Chloropicophyceae, including <i>C. primus</i>, produce astaxanthin<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Lopes Dos Santos, A. et al. Chloropicophyceae, a new class of picophytoplanktonic prasinophytes. Sci. Rep. 7, 14019 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1204">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Lopes Dos Santos, A., Gourvil, P., Rodriguez, F., Garrido, J. L. &amp; Vaulot, D. Photosynthetic pigments of oceanic Chlorophyta belonging to prasinophytes clade VII. J. Phycol. 52, 148–155 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1207">39</a></sup>. On the other hand, carotenoids characteristic of the prasino-3 group (prasinoxanthin, dihydrolutein, uriolide, micromonal, and micromonol) are found in the Mamiellophyceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Jeffrey, S. W., Wright, S. W. &amp; Zapata, M. Microalgal classes and their signature pigments. In Phytoplankton Pigments: Characterization, Chemotaxonomy and Applications in Oceanography (eds Llewellyn, C. A., Egeland, E. S., Johnsen, G. &amp; Roy, S.) (Cambridge Univ. Press, 2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1211">42</a></sup>.</p><p>We identified the complete set of <i>Chloropicon</i> genes responsible for carotenoid biosynthesis (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM15">12</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM1">5</a>). As in land plant and core chlorophytes, two desaturases (Pds and Zds) and two <i>cis</i>-<i>trans</i> isomerases (Z-Iso and CrtIso) catalyze the four steps of dehydrogenation that are required for converting 15-<i>cis</i> phytoene into all-<i>trans</i> lycopene. In addition, there is a gene encoding a bacterial/fungal-type phytoene desaturase (CrtI), an enzyme performing multiple dehydrogenation steps. All members of the Mamiellophyceae are missing a number of genes in the pathway typically found in both chlorophytes and streptophytes.</p><p>The proteins known to participate directly in astaxanthin synthesis, namely beta-carotene ketolase (Bkt) and beta-carotene hydroxylase (Chyb), are encoded by single-copy genes in <i>Chloropicon</i>. The order in which they function in <i>H</i>. <i>pluvialis</i> and <i>C</i>. <i>zofingiensis</i> is still uncertain<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Solovchenko, A. E. Recent breakthroughs in the biology of astaxanthin accumulation by microalgal cell. Photosynth. Res. 125, 437–449 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1259">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Liu, J. et al. Utilization of cane molasses towards cost-saving astaxanthin production by a Chlorella zofingiensis mutant. J. Appl. Phycol. 25, 1447–1456 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1262">41</a></sup>. Considering that only <i>chyb</i> has been retained in the Mamiellophyceae, we speculate that its protein product is required to synthesize zeaxanthin, a precursor of both violaxanthin and neoxanthin; hence, assuming that the <i>Chloropicon</i> Chyb has the same substrate specificity, we propose that subsequent ketolation of zeaxanthin by Bkt yields astaxanthin (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM1">5</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Thiamine biosynthesis genes</h3><p>We inferred that <i>Chloropicon</i> has the ability to synthesize thiamine (vitamin B1) (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM16">13</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM1">6</a>). Considering that the availability of exogenous vitamins is limited in open oceans, the retention of this biosynthesis pathway may help explain its ubiquitous presence of <i>Chloropicon</i> across the globe. In the form of thiamine diphosphate (TPP), vitamin B1 is an essential cofactor for energy generation and general metabolism that is produced via the coupling of pyrimidine and thiazole precursors, namely hydroxymethylpyrimidine pyrophosphate (HMP-PP) and hydroxyethyl-thiazole phosphate (HET-P) or 5-(2-hydroxyethyl)-4-methyl-1,3-thiazole-2-carboxylic acid (cHET-P)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Helliwell, K. E. The roles of B vitamins in phytoplankton nutrition: new perspectives and prospects. New Phytol. 216, 62–68 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1295">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Paerl, R. W. et al. Carboxythiazole is a key microbial nutrient currency and critical component of thiamin biosynthesis. Sci. Rep. 8, 5940 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1298">44</a></sup>. Catalyzed by the bifunctional enzyme TH1, this reaction results in thiamine monophosphate, a precursor that is subsequently converted to TPP by the kinase TPK. The pyrimidine moiety is produced in reactions catalyzed by ThiC and TH1, whereas the thiazole group is synthesized by Thi4<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Helliwell, K. E. The roles of B vitamins in phytoplankton nutrition: new perspectives and prospects. New Phytol. 216, 62–68 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1303">43</a></sup>.</p><p>In <i>Ostreococcus</i> and <i>Micromonas</i>, <i>thiC</i>, <i>thi4</i> and <i>th1</i> are pseudogenes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Helliwell, K. E. The roles of B vitamins in phytoplankton nutrition: new perspectives and prospects. New Phytol. 216, 62–68 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1326">43</a></sup>. To survive, these picoeukaryotes are dependent on exogenous vitamin B1 or related micronutrients supplied by B1-synthesizing marine bacteria or eukaryotic phytoplankton<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Paerl, R. W. et al. Carboxythiazole is a key microbial nutrient currency and critical component of thiamin biosynthesis. Sci. Rep. 8, 5940 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1330">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Paerl, R. W. et al. Use of plankton-derived vitamin B1 precursors, especially thiazole-related precursor, by key marine picoeukaryotic phytoplankton. ISME J. 11, 753–765 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1333">45</a></sup>. It has been recently shown that the thiazole precursor cHET is a key micronutrient in the Mamiellophyceae and that its utilization is dependent upon the activity of the kinase ThiM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Paerl, R. W. et al. Carboxythiazole is a key microbial nutrient currency and critical component of thiamin biosynthesis. Sci. Rep. 8, 5940 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1337">44</a></sup>. A <i>Chloropicon</i> homolog of this protein could not be detected by BlastP searches.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Catabolism of branched-chain amino acids</h3><p>In plants, leucine, isoleucine, and valine are known to play a crucial role as alternative sources of energy under carbohydrate starvation or prolonged darkness<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Peng, C., Uygun, S., Shiu, S.-H. &amp; Last, R. L. The impact of the branched-chain ketoacid dehydrogenase complex on amino acid homeostasis in Arabidopsis. Plant Physiol. 169, 1807–1820 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1352">46</a></sup> (see Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM1">7</a> for the branched-chain amino acids, BCAA, degradation pathways). This auxiliary supply of energy is generated in the mitochondrion. The <i>Chloropicon</i> genome potentially encodes all the proteins necessary to degrade the three BCAAs and similarly, complete BCAA catabolic pathways were inferred for the trebouxiophytes <i>Helicosporidium, Auxenochlorella</i>, and <i>Coccomyxa</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM1">7</a> and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM17">14</a>). In the case of the four other examined core chlorophytes, only one enzyme (enoyl-CoA hydratase, K07511) in the valine and isoleucine catabolic pathways was not detected using GhostKOALA. In contrast, numerous proteins in the degradation pathways of the three BCAAs were not retrieved in the Mamiellophyceae.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">A metabolic pathway unique to <i>Chloropicon</i>
                        </h3><p>Among the proteins unique to <i>Chloropicon</i>, we uncovered the complete set of enzymes performing the degradation of propionate via the 2-methylcitrate cycle (2-MCC)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Watson, E. et al. Metabolic network rewiring of propionate flux compensates vitamin B12 deficiency in C. elegans. eLife 5, e17670 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1390">47</a></sup>, namely ACS, PrpC, PrpD, AcnB, and PrpB (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#Fig6">6</a> and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM18">15</a>). The gene encoding PrpF was also identified, albeit this enzyme is not likely to function in an alternative branch of the 2-MCC due to the absence of the upstream gene product (AcnD). To ensure that the 2-MCC was not previously overlooked in the Chloroplastida, we examined all green algal genomes sequenced to date using GhostKOALA and did not identify PrpC, PrpB, and PrpF. Although PrpD was retrieved in the <i>Ostreococcus</i> and <i>Micromonas</i> genera, the latter protein sequences were highly divergent from that of <i>Chloropicon</i> (<i>E</i>-value ≥ 1.0E-10), an unanticipated result considering the very strong level of similarity (<i>E</i>-value = 0) observed for the <i>Chloropicon</i> and mamiellophycean orthologs of the bifunctional AcnB enzyme (aconitate hydratase in the TCA cycle and 2-methylisocitrate dehydratase in the 2-MCC). Congruent with BlastP searches, phylogenetic trees inferred from the <i>Chloropicon</i> PrpB, PrpC, PrpD, and PrpF proteins identified different bacteria and/or non-photosynthetic eukaryotes as their closest relatives (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM1">8</a>), highlighting complex evolutionary histories for the genes encoding these proteins which likely involved horizontal transfer events from bacteria and multiple losses in eukaryotic lineages. Reminiscent of the gene organization found in bacteria<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Dolan, S. K. et al. Loving the poison: the methylcitrate cycle and bacterial pathogenesis. Microbiology 164, 251–259 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1426">48</a></sup>, <i>prpB</i>, <i>prpD,</i> and <i>prpC</i> are closely linked on chromosome V of the <i>Chloropicon</i> genome (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM5">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Fig. 6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-12014-x/MediaObjects/41467_2019_12014_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-12014-x/MediaObjects/41467_2019_12014_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="585" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p><i>Chloropicon</i> proteins predicted to participate in the degradation of propionate via the modified β-oxidation and 2-MCC pathways. Supplementary Data 15 report the loci of the encoding genes, the presence/absence of homologs in other green algae and additional information, including expression data and predicted subcellular localization. PrpC, 2-methylcitrate synthase [EC:2.3.3.5]; PrpD, 2-methylcitrate dehydratase [EC:4.2.1.79]; AcnB, aconitate hydratase 2/2-methylisocitrate dehydratase [EC:4.2.1.99]; PrpB, methylisocitrate lyase [EC:4.1.3.30]; PrpF, 2-methylaconitate <i>cis-trans</i>-isomerase [EC:5.3.3.-]; ACOX, acyl-CoA dehydrogenase [EC:1.3.8.7]; ECH, enoyl-CoA hydratase [EC:4.2.1.17]; HADH, 3-hydroxyisobutyryl-CoA hydrolase [EC:3.1.2.4]; HDH, 3-hydroxyisobutyrate dehydrogenase/3-hydroxypropionate dehydrogenase [EC:1.1.1.31]; MMSDH, malonate-semialdehyde dehydrogenase (acetylating)/methylmalonate-semialdehyde dehydrogenase [EC:1.2.1.18 1.2.1.27]; ACS, acetyl-CoA synthetase [EC:6.2.1.1]</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec13" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec13-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec13">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec13-content"><p>Like mamiellalean genomes, the <i>Chloropicon</i> genome has a small size and is densely packed with coding sequences, with a minimum of gene redundancy;
