however, extensive differences are seen at the gene content and gene arrangement levels. <i>Chloropicon</i> exhibits genes/pathways that are present in core chlorophytes but not in mamiellaleans, and conversely, mamiellaleans exhibits genes/pathways that are not present in <i>Chloropicon</i>, suggesting that the two groups of picoalgae adopted different strategies to adapt to their oceanic environments. Key genes belonging to the former category include those encoding pigment-binding proteins of light-harvesting complexes, proteins required for synthesis of carotenoid and thiamine, and enzymes participating in degradation of BCAAs.</p><p>The common ancestor of the Chloropicophyceae and Mamiellophyceae emerged about 950 Mya<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Leliaert, F. et al. Chloroplast phylogenomic analyses reveal the deepest-branching lineage of the Chlorophyta, Palmophyllophyceae class. nov. Sci. Rep. 6, 25367 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1490">49</a></sup> and like the algae belonging to the sister class of the Mamiellophyceae (Pyramimonadophyceae), was presumably a scaly flagellate with a large cell body and a complex internal morphology that enabled a phago-mixotrophic mode of nutrition<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Burns, J. A., Paasch, A., Narechania, A. &amp; Kim, E. Comparative genomics of a bacterivorous green alga reveals evolutionary causalities and consequences of phago-mixotrophic mode of nutrition. Genome Biol. Evol. 7, 3047–3061 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1494">50</a></sup>. A draft genome of the phagophotrophic <i>Cymbomonas tetramitiformis</i> highlighted a considerably greater genome size (850–1200 Mb) and gene repertoire (37,366 gene models) compared to those of <i>Chloropicon</i> and the Mamiellales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Burns, J. A., Paasch, A., Narechania, A. &amp; Kim, E. Comparative genomics of a bacterivorous green alga reveals evolutionary causalities and consequences of phago-mixotrophic mode of nutrition. Genome Biol. Evol. 7, 3047–3061 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1504">50</a></sup>. Our study thus reinforces the view that several independent events of genome minimizations, entailing differential gene losses, occurred during prasinophyte diversification.</p><p>Analyses of allelic ratios at polymorphic sites and of chromosome coverage depths revealed that all 20 <i>Chloropicon</i> chromosomes, except the trisomic chromosome XVII, are present in two copies. Further supporting this conclusion is the finding that the DNA content previously estimated for <i>Chloropicon</i> cells by flow cytometry predicted a genome size of about 45 Mb, which is twice larger than our genome assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Lopes Dos Santos, A. et al. Chloropicophyceae, a new class of picophytoplanktonic prasinophytes. Sci. Rep. 7, 14019 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1517">30</a></sup>. While diploid strains of <i>C. reinhardtii</i> that reproduce asexually can be generated in the laboratory, a predominantly diploid phase of growth (i.e. diplontic life cycle) has not been observed among natural isolates of unicellular green algae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="von Dassow, P. &amp; Montresor, M. Unveiling the mysteries of phytoplankton life cycles: patterns and opportunities behind complexity. J. Plankton Res. 33, 3–12 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1524">51</a></sup>, but it is prevalent in other groups of unicellular algae such as the diatoms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Otto, S. P. &amp; Gerstein, A. C. The evolution of haploidy and diploidy. Curr. Biol. 18, R1121–R1124 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1529">52</a></sup>. In green algae, diploid vegetative cells have been documented solely for ulvophytes with a haplo-diplontic life cycle<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Otto, S. P. &amp; Gerstein, A. C. The evolution of haploidy and diploidy. Curr. Biol. 18, R1121–R1124 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1533">52</a></sup>.</p><p>Despite its higher metabolic cost, diploidy (i.e. a life cycle with a predominantly diploid growth phase) has been traditionally viewed as evolutionary favored over haploidy. By allowing the masking of nearly all deleterious mutations arising in the genome, diploidy has been proposed to confer higher organismal fitness and a larger pool of mutant alleles that may help adaptation to new environments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Otto, S. P. &amp; Gerstein, A. C. The evolution of haploidy and diploidy. Curr. Biol. 18, R1121–R1124 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1540">52</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Mable, B. K. &amp; Otto, S. P. The evolution of life cycles with haploid and diploid phases. Bioessays 20, 453–462 (1998)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1543">53</a></sup>. However, given that more mutations arise in diploids than haploids and that selection is less efficient, theoretical studies have shown that a dominant diploid phase is disadvantageous under certain circumstances, e.g. when the environment is static, and is tolerated as long as deleterious mutations are masked and as long as there is enough sex and recombination in the population. By contrast, newly arising deleterious mutations are more efficiently eliminated in haploids and beneficial mutations spread more easily, increasing fitness at equilibrium. Furthermore, haploid cells may have a nutritional advantage over diploid cells because they are often smaller than diploids and thus have a higher surface area to volume ratio; in the yeast <i>Saccharomyces cerevisiae</i>, it has been experimentally shown that haploids are more suitable than diploids for some conditions of nutrient limitations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Otto, S. P. &amp; Gerstein, A. C. The evolution of haploidy and diploidy. Curr. Biol. 18, R1121–R1124 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1550">52</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Mable, B. K. &amp; Otto, S. P. The evolution of life cycles with haploid and diploid phases. Bioessays 20, 453–462 (1998)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1553">53</a></sup>. In this context, it is noteworthy that different isolates of <i>Chloropicon roscoffensis</i> were shown to differ in DNA content and that two strains from the low genome size (20 Mb) group also display a 25% smaller average cell diameter compared to strains from the high genome size (42 Mb) group<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Lopes Dos Santos, A. et al. Chloropicophyceae, a new class of picophytoplanktonic prasinophytes. Sci. Rep. 7, 14019 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1560">30</a></sup>.</p><p>Only a few green algae, including the prasinophyte <i>Nephroselmis olivacea</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Suda, S., Watanabe Makoto, M. &amp; Inouye, I. Evidence for sexual reproduction in the primitive green alga Nephroselmis olivacea (Prasinophyceae). J. Phycol. 25, 596–600 (1989)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1570">54</a></sup>, have been observed to engage in sex in nature or laboratory conditions. In line with the idea that sexual reproduction is nearly universal in eukaryotes, genomics and/or population genetics studies algae sequenced have revealed indirect evidence for the capacity of sexual reproduction in most of the green so far<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Derelle, E. et al. Genome analysis of the smallest free-living eukaryote Ostreococcus tauri unveils many unique features. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 103, 11647–11652 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1574">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Worden, A. Z. et al. Green evolution and dynamic adaptations revealed by genomes of the marine picoeukaryotes Micromonas. Science 324, 268–272 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1577">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Blanc, G. et al. The Chlorella variabilis NC64A genome reveals adaptation to photosymbiosis, coevolution with viruses, and cryptic sex. Plant Cell 22, 2943–2955 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1580">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Hovde, B. T. et al. Genomic characterization reveals significant divergence within Chlorella sorokiniana (Chlorellales, Trebouxiophyceae). Algal Res. 35, 449–461 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1583">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Blanc-Mathieu, R. et al. Population genomics of picophytoplankton unveils novel chromosome hypervariability. Sci. Adv. 3, e1700239 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1586">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Grimsley, N., Pequin, B., Bachy, C., Moreau, H. &amp; Piganeau, G. Cryptic sex in the smallest eukaryotic marine green alga. Mol. Biol. Evol. 27, 47–54 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1589">55</a></sup>, including <i>Chloropicon</i>. The conservation of genes related to meiosis/sex and flagellar structure and maintenance (flagella play a role in the recognition of mating partners) suggests that <i>Chloropicon</i> had a past history of sex or has retained the ability to produce haploid gametes whose fusion gives rise to new diploid genotypes with purged deleterious alleles. Our data on the expression of sialyltransferase genes also raise the possibility that there exists a life-cycle phase in which cells exhibit sialylated scales or scale-like structures. This phase might be linked to sexual reproduction, given that a sialyltransferase activity believed to be important for cell-cell interaction has been identified on the external surface of gametes in <i>Chlamydomonas moewusii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Colombino, L. F., Bosmann, H. B. &amp; McLean, R. J. Cell surface localization of the sialyltransferase ectoenzyme system during the Chlamydomonas mating reaction. Exp. Cell Res. 112, 25–30 (1978)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1602">56</a></sup>.</p><p>Assuming that <i>Chloropicon</i> is heterothallic (mating occurring between gametes with genetically determined mating-types), one would expect that the SDR would be diploid in the same way as vegetative diploids of <i>Chlamydomonas</i> (with one copy of each <i>mt</i><sup><i>+</i></sup> and <i>mt</i><sup><i>−</i></sup> loci) and that, as documented for heterothallic volvocine algae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Coelho, S. M., Gueno, J., Lipinska, A. P., Cock, J. M. &amp; Umen, J. G. UV chromosomes and haploid sexual systems. Trends Plant. Sci. 23, 794–807 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1628">34</a></sup>, the dominant sex-determining gene <i>MID</i> gene would be located within a genomic region enriched in indels and SNPs. Our failure to identify a region with these characteristics may be interpreted as indicating that the <i>Chloropicon mt</i><sup><i>+</i></sup> and <i>mt</i><sup><i>−</i></sup> loci are short and highly similar to one another. However, such an exceptionally high level of similarity seems unlikely considering our current knowledge about SDR architecture<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Coelho, S. M., Gueno, J., Lipinska, A. P., Cock, J. M. &amp; Umen, J. G. UV chromosomes and haploid sexual systems. Trends Plant. Sci. 23, 794–807 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1647">34</a></sup> and for this reason, the hypothesis that <i>Chloropicon</i> is homothallic (self-fertile) cannot be ruled out. In volvocine lineages, homothallism repeatedly evolved from heterothallic ancestors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Hanschen, E. R., Herron, M. D., Wiens, J. J., Nozaki, H. &amp; Michod, R. E. Repeated evolution and reversibility of self-fertilization in the volvocine green algae. Evolution 72, 386–398 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1655">57</a></sup> and for a homothallic species of <i>Volvox</i>, it has been shown that epigenetic control of expression of the dominant <i>MID</i> is probably involved in the formation of male and female gametes within a single clonal culture, although no information is currently available on the genomic region corresponding to the <i>MT</i> locus of heterothallic relatives<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Yamamoto, K. et al. Molecular evolutionary analysis of a gender-limited MID ortholog from the homothallic species Volvox africanus with male and monoecious spheroids. PLoS ONE 12, e0180313 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1668">58</a></sup>.</p><p>Aneuploidy is a widespread phenomenon in unicellulars such as <i>S. cerevisiae</i> and the primarily asexual fungus <i>Candida albicans</i> (an heterozygous diploid like <i>C. primus</i>) that imposes a burden on the cell but provides a fitness advantage under certain growth or stressful conditions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Yona, A. H. et al. Chromosomal duplication is a transient evolutionary solution to stress. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 109, 21010–21015 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1684">59</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Ene, I. V. et al. Global analysis of mutations driving microevolution of a heterozygous diploid fungal pathogen. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 115, E8688–E8697 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1687">60</a></sup>. <i>Chloropicon</i> might have gained a third copy of chromosome XVII during the period of asexual growth following isolation of the original <i>Chloropicon</i> CCMP1205 strain in 1965. Additional <i>C</i>. <i>primus</i> isolates will need to be investigated to determine the time of emergence of this trisomy.</p><p>The significant deviations from the 1:1 allelic ratio observed on chromosome IV and other chromosomal regions might be the result of biased gene conversion events (LOH events) arising from mitotic recombination, followed by segregation of distinct genotypes during further growth of <i>Chloropicon</i> CCMP1205. In asexual diploid eukaryotes, LOH is thought to provide a mechanism for removing deleterious mutations and thus compensate for a lack of sex<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ene, I. V. et al. Global analysis of mutations driving microevolution of a heterozygous diploid fungal pathogen. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 115, E8688–E8697 (2018)." href="#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1710">60</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Dale, A. L. et al. Mitotic recombination and rapid genome evolution in the invasive forest pathogen Phytophthora ramorum. mBio 10, e02452–02418 (2019)." href="#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1710_1">61</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Magwene, P. M. et al. Outcrossing, mitotic recombination, and life-history trade-offs shape genome evolution in Saccharomyces cerevisiae. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 1987–1992 (2011)." href="#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1710_2">62</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Mandegar, M. A. &amp; Otto, S. P. Mitotic recombination counteracts the benefits of genetic segregation. Proc. Biol. Sci. 274, 1301–1307 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1713">63</a></sup>.</p><p>Among the proteins unique to <i>Chloropicon</i>, we uncovered the complete set of enzymes performing the degradation of propionyl-CoA via the 2-MCC. Propionate, in the form of propionyl-CoA, is a toxic metabolite that is produced by several pathways: breakdown of several amino acids (methionine, threonine, and the BCAAs isoleucine and valine), β-oxidation of odd-chain fatty acids, and catabolism of branched fatty acids, such as phytanic acid which is derived from the degradation of chlorophyll. Propionyl-CoA has been shown to interfere with enzymes of the central metabolism<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Otzen, C., Bardl, B., Jacobsen, I. D., Nett, M. &amp; Brock, M. Candida albicans utilizes a modified beta-oxidation pathway for the degradation of toxic propionyl-CoA. J. Biol. Chem. 289, 8151–8169 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1723">64</a></sup> and to inhibit polyketide synthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Zhang, Y. Q. &amp; Keller, N. P. Blockage of methylcitrate cycle inhibits polyketide production in Aspergillus nidulans. Mol. Microbiol. 52, 541–550 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1727">65</a></sup>. In many prokaryotes and some eukaryotes, propionate also serves as a carbon source<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Dolan, S. K. et al. Loving the poison: the methylcitrate cycle and bacterial pathogenesis. Microbiology 164, 251–259 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1731">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Otzen, C., Bardl, B., Jacobsen, I. D., Nett, M. &amp; Brock, M. Candida albicans utilizes a modified beta-oxidation pathway for the degradation of toxic propionyl-CoA. J. Biol. Chem. 289, 8151–8169 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1734">64</a></sup>. Organisms have evolved a variety of mechanisms to convert propionyl-CoA into products that can be further metabolized via the TCA cycle<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Watson, E. et al. Metabolic network rewiring of propionate flux compensates vitamin B12 deficiency in C. elegans. eLife 5, e17670 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1738">47</a></sup>: a propionate carboxylation pathway dependent on vitamin B12 that produces succinyl-CoA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Helliwell, K. E., Wheeler, G. L., Leptos, K. C., Goldstein, R. E. &amp; Smith, A. G. Insights into the evolution of vitamin B12 auxotrophy from sequenced algal genomes. Mol. Biol. Evol. 28, 2921–2933 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1743">66</a></sup>, a modified β-oxidation pathway leading to acetyl-CoA and CO<sub>2</sub>, and the 2-MCC pathway converting propionate into succinate and pyruvate.</p><p>It remains unclear whether the 2-MCC is the only metabolic route available to <i>Chloropicon</i> for propionyl-CoA detoxification. We identified candidate genes that potentially encode all the proteins catalyzing the reactions of the modified β-oxidation pathway (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#Fig6">6</a> and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM18">15</a>); however, all are also required for BCAA catabolism (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM17">14</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM1">7</a>) and contrary to the model plant <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Lucas, K. A., Filley, J. R., Erb, J. M., Graybill, E. R. &amp; Hawes, J. W. Peroxisomal metabolism of propionic acid and isobutyric acid in plants. J. Biol. Chem. 282, 24980–24989 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1770">67</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Schertl, P., Danne, L. &amp; Braun, H.-P. 3-Hydroxyisobutyrate dehydrogenase is involved in both, valine and isoleucine degradation in Arabidopsis thaliana. Plant Physiol. 175, 51–61 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1773">68</a></sup>, PredAlgo and TargetP analyses predicted that the putative enoyl-CoA hydratase (ECH, K07511) responsible for the production of the key metabolite β-hydroxypropionate is located in the mitochondrion instead of the peroxisome.</p><p>Considering the scarcity of nutrients available in open oceans compared to coastal waters, the ability to recycle propionate for other metabolic purposes such as a source of carbon and energy could confer a clear benefit to <i>Chloropicon</i>. To our knowledge, the colorless obligatory-heterotrophic trebouxiophyte <i>Prototheca zopfii</i> is the sole green alga that has been reported to utilize propionate as carbon source, and in this case, this organic acid appears to be metabolized by the modified β-oxidation pathway<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Callely, A. G. &amp; Lloyd, D. The metabolism of propionate in the colourless alga, Prototheca zopfii. Biochem. J. 92, 338–345 (1964)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1787">69</a></sup>. In contrast, no propionate catabolic pathway appears to be present in the Mamiellophyceae; given their inability to catabolize BCAAs using the pathways typically found in eukaryotes, these prasinophytes can perhaps dispense with propionyl-CoA detoxification.</p><p>As reported for plants, BCAA degradation might contribute to cell fitness in the Chloropicophyceae and core chlorophytes under carbohydrate starvation or prolonged darkness. The apparent absence of the typical BCAA catabolic pathways in the Mamiellophyceae is intriguing; perhaps these prasinophytes possess alternative pathways to degrade BCAAs that are not available to most eukaryotes, such as the Ehrlich pathway used by <i>S. cerevisiae</i> during fermentation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Dickinson, J. R., Harrison, S. J., Dickinson, J. A. &amp; Hewlins, M. J. E. An Investigation of the metabolism of isoleucine to active amyl alcohol in Saccharomyces cerevisiae. J. Biol. Chem. 275, 10937–10942 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1797">70</a></sup>. Alternatively, mamiellophyceans might be less dependent than <i>Chloropicon</i> and core chlorophytes upon BCAA catabolism under nutrient- or light-limiting conditions if they are more efficient at producing reserves of energy in the forms of carbohydrates or lipids or at harvesting energy from alternative metabolites. In this context, it is worth reporting that our genome analyses predict that <i>Chloropicon</i> utilizes a more limited range of intermediates than mamiellophyceans during anaerobic fermentation metabolism<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Hirooka, S. et al. Acidophilic green algal genome provides insights into adaptation to an acidic environment. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 114, E8304–E8313 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1807">14</a></sup> (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM19">16</a>) and that unlike mamiellophyceans<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Worden, A. Z. et al. Green evolution and dynamic adaptations revealed by genomes of the marine picoeukaryotes Micromonas. Science 324, 268–272 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1815">6</a></sup>, it cannot use a C4-like mechanism to concentrate CO<sub>2</sub> due to the absence of the gene encoding a pyruvate orthophosphate dikinase (EC 2.7.9.1).</p><p>The differences in Lhcb proteins between the Mamiellophyceae and Chloropicophyceae are possibly linked to differing pigment compositions. These algal classes exhibit distinct carotenoid profiles and unlike the Mamiellophyceae, the Chloropicophyceae lack the chlorophyll <i>c</i> derivative Mg-DVP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Jeffrey, S. W., Wright, S. W. &amp; Zapata, M. Microalgal classes and their signature pigments. In Phytoplankton Pigments: Characterization, Chemotaxonomy and Applications in Oceanography (eds Llewellyn, C. A., Egeland, E. S., Johnsen, G. &amp; Roy, S.) (Cambridge Univ. Press, 2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1827">42</a></sup>. The capacity to produce astaxanthin may help <i>Chloropicon</i> to thrive under high light intensity. This ketocarotenoid is thought to confer protection by absorbing excess light and quenching reactive oxygen species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Roth, M. S. et al. Chromosome-level genome assembly and transcriptome of the green alga Chromochloris zofingiensis illuminates astaxanthin production. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 114, E4296–E4305 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1834">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Solovchenko, A. E. Recent breakthroughs in the biology of astaxanthin accumulation by microalgal cell. Photosynth. Res. 125, 437–449 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1837">40</a></sup>.</p><p>Another key feature of the <i>Chloropicon</i> genome is the large number of guanylyl cyclase genes, which suggests an important role in cellular signaling and regulation of gene expression<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Düner, M., Lambertz, J., Mügge, C. &amp; Hemschemeier, A. The soluble guanylate cyclase CYG12 is required for the acclimation to hypoxia and trophic regimes in Chlamydomonas reinhardtii. Plant J. 93, 311–337 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1847">71</a></sup>. In <i>C</i>. <i>reinhardtii</i>, studies of two of the six soluble isoforms of guanylate cyclase present in this alga showed that one is involved in the regulation of nitrate assimilatory genes and proteins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="de Montaigu, A., Sanz-Luque, E., Galvan, A. &amp; Fernandez, E. A soluble guanylate cyclase mediates negative signaling by ammonium on expression of nitrate reductase in Chlamydomonas. Plant Cell 22, 1532–1548 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1857">72</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Sanz-Luque, E., Chamizo-Ampudia, A., Llamas, A., Galvan, A. &amp; Fernandez, E. Understanding nitrate assimilation and its regulation in microalgae. Front. Plant Sci. 6, 899 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1860">73</a></sup> and that the other is required for acclimation to hypoxia and other conditions that impact the cellular energy status<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Düner, M., Lambertz, J., Mügge, C. &amp; Hemschemeier, A. The soluble guanylate cyclase CYG12 is required for the acclimation to hypoxia and trophic regimes in Chlamydomonas reinhardtii. Plant J. 93, 311–337 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1865">71</a></sup>.</p><p>The <i>Chloropicon</i> genome sequence provides a solid foundation for future studies on the special attributes enabling the Chloropicophyceae to play key roles in phytoplankton communities of tropical open oceanic waters. It will be important to characterize at the genome-wide level the significant genetic diversity uncovered in this class<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Lopes Dos Santos, A. et al. Diversity and oceanic distribution of prasinophytes clade VII, the dominant group of green algae in oceanic waters. ISME J. 11, 512–528 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1875">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Lopes Dos Santos, A. et al. Chloropicophyceae, a new class of picophytoplanktonic prasinophytes. Sci. Rep. 7, 14019 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1878">30</a></sup> and search for genes/pathways linked to species or ecotype distributions.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec14" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec14-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec14">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec14-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Cell culture and DNA/RNA extraction</h3><p><i>Chloropicon primus</i> strain CCMP 1205 (isolated in 1965 from the Atlantic Ocean) was obtained from the National Center for Marine Algae and Microbiota (East Bootbay, ME, USA) where it is maintained as a cryopreserved, genetically pure culture after it was re-cloned in October 1987. For DNA extraction, cells were grown synchronously in L1 medium at 18 °C under 12-hour light/dark cycles and harvested after 2–3 weeks. For RNA extraction, independent cultures were grown in the same medium under synchronized and continuous light conditions; synchronized cells were harvested 2 and 8 h after the onset of the light (L2 and L8) and dark (D2 and D8) cycles, whereas cells grown in continuous light were harvested at the exponential and stationary growth phases. Total DNA and RNA were extracted using the HP Plant DNA Mini and Total RNA II kits (Omega Bio-tek, Norcross, GA, USA), respectively. RNA integrity was monitored using a Bioanalyzer (Agilent Technologies, Santa Clara, CA, USA).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">DNA and RNA sequencing</h3><p>The <i>Chloropicon</i> genome was sequenced using a combination of high-throughput short and long reads generated on the Illumina (San Diego, CA, USA) and Pacific Biosciences (Menlo Park, CA, USA) platforms, respectively. Paired-end (average insert size of 522 bp) and mate-pair (average insert size of 1757 bp) libraries were prepared using the Illumina TruSeq DNA kit and the mate-pair protocol of Birol et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Birol, I. et al. Assembling the 20 Gb white spruce (Picea glauca) genome from whole-genome shotgun sequencing data. Bioinformatics 29, 1492–1497 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1907">74</a></sup>, and MiSeq sequencing at the Plateforme d’Analyses Génomiques of Université Laval (Québec, QC, Canada) yielded 9.6 million paired-end reads of 250 bp and 6.0 million mate-pair reads of 300 bp. For long-read sequencing, DNA was sheared by centrifugation in Covaris g-TUBEs (Woburn, MA, USA), libraries were prepared with the DNA Template Prep Kit 3.0 using the 20-kb protocol (Pacific Biosciences), and DNA was sequenced on the PacBio RS II platform (four SMRT cells, P6-C4 chemistry) at the University of Michigan DNA Sequencing Core (Ann Arbor, MI), providing 1.3 GB of reads, with polymerase and subread N50s of 7.99 and 4.89 kb, respectively (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM1">9</a>).</p><p>A normalized RNAseq library was prepared by the Plateforme d’Analyses Génomiques of Université Laval from a mixture containing equal amounts of RNAs isolated from the L2, L8, D2, and D8 synchronized cells and exponential and stationary cultures grown in continuous light. The library was constructed using the Illumina TruSeq RNA kit and the normalization protocol of Zhulidov et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Zhulidov, P. A. et al. Simple cDNA normalization using kamchatka crab duplex-specific nuclease. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, e37 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1917">75</a></sup> and sequenced on the Illumina HiSEQ 2000 platform (300 cycles) at the McGill University and Génome Québec Innovation Centre (Montréal, Canada), yielding 22,223,345 paired-end reads.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Genome assembly</h3><p>Independent de novo assemblies were generated from short and long Illumina and PacBio read datasets and then merged to produce a high-quality consensus draft using overlap-consensus-approaches followed by manual curation/inspection of discrepancies. Low-quality bases in the Illumina reads were removed with PRINSEQ 0.20.4 and adapter sequences were discarded using FASTX_CLIPPER from the FASTX-Toolkit 0.0.14 package. Pre-assembly of overlapping paired-end reads was performed with FLASH. Assembly was performed using Ray 2.3.1 and a kmer value of 61 on the paired-end and mate-pair datasets, with reads overlapped with FLASH used as singletons. PacBio assemblies were performed using MHAP 1.5 and HGAP3 as implemented in the Celera assembler version 8.3RC1 and SMRT-Analysis Portal 2.3.0, respectively. For the Celera assembly, FASTQ reads were generated from the bax.h5 files with DEXTRACTOR, converted to Celera format with the fastqToCA tool from the Celera package, quality-trimmed by overlap using runCA, and then assembled with MHAP 1.5.</p><p>Illumina Ray and PacBio HGAP3 assemblies were merged using the alignment tool for large DNA fragments implemented in Sequencher 5.4.1 (Gene Codes Corporation, Ann Arbor, MI, USA). Discrepancies in the consensus sequence were resolved manually using the editing tool of Sequencher and the basecalling of the Illumina contigs, and when indels were encountered, sequences of the long alleles were integrated into the consensus. Additional overlaps between the polished Ray/HGAP3 contigs and the Celera MHAP assembly were identified using dot plot analyses as implemented in dottup from the EMBOSS 6.4.0 package. Because the Celera assembly produced longer contigs albeit with more inaccurate basecalling, the latter contigs were only used for scaffolding the Ray/HGAP3 assemblies, with the corresponding basecalling in the scaffolded regions curated manually.</p><p>The absence of contaminants in the assembled contigs was confirmed by taxonomized BLAST homology searches against the NCBI nr/taxonomy databases using runTaxonomizedBLAST.pl.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Genome annotations</h3><p>Ribosomal and transfer RNA genes were identified using RNAmmer 1.2 and tRNAscan-SE 1.3.1, respectively. Protein-coding genes were predicted using the MAKER 2.32 and BRAKER 1.8 pipelines. For BRAKER 1.8, alignments of Illumina RNAseq reads against the assembled genome were generated in BAM format using HISAT2 2.0.1 and default parameters. To detect protein-coding genes improperly predicted or missed in previous analyses, RNAseq data were mapped independently on the assembled genome with PASS 2.23 using the default mapping seed (111111101111111) with a seed step of 3, the homopolymer flag turned on (-flc 1), an identity of 90%, a filtering length of 50 nt, and the gap mode set to 2. In addition, protein datasets derived from <i>Auxenochlorella protothecoides</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Gao, C. et al. Oil accumulation mechanisms of the oleaginous microalga Chlorella protothecoides revealed through its genome, transcriptomes, and proteomes. BMC Genomics 15, 582 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1945">76</a></sup>, <i>Bathycoccus prasinos</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Moreau, H. et al. Gene functionalities and genome structure in Bathycoccus prasinos reflect cellular specializations at the base of the green lineage. Genome Biol. 13, R74 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1951">5</a></sup>, <i>Chlorella variabilis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Blanc, G. et al. The Chlorella variabilis NC64A genome reveals adaptation to photosymbiosis, coevolution with viruses, and cryptic sex. Plant Cell 22, 2943–2955 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1957">13</a></sup>, <i>C. reinhardtii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Merchant, S. S. et al. The Chlamydomonas genome reveals the evolution of key animal and plant functions. Science 318, 245–250 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1964">9</a></sup>, <i>Coccomyxa subellipsoidea</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Blanc, G. et al. The genome of the polar eukaryotic microalga Coccomyxa subellipsoidea reveals traits of cold adaptation. Genome Biol. 13, R39 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1970">12</a></sup>, <i>Gonium pectorale</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Hanschen, E. R. et al. The Gonium pectorale genome demonstrates co-option of cell cycle regulation during the evolution of multicellularity. Nat. Commun. 7, 11370 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1976">8</a></sup>, <i>Helicosporidium</i> sp.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Pombert, J. F., Blouin, N. A., Lane, C., Boucias, D. &amp; Keeling, P. J. A lack of parasitic reduction in the obligate parasitic green alga Helicosporidium. PLoS Genet. 10, e1004355 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1983">77</a></sup>, <i>Klebsormidium flaccidum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Hori, K. et al. Klebsormidium flaccidum genome reveals primary factors for plant terrestrial adaptation. Nat. Commun. 5, 3978 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1990">15</a></sup>, <i>Micromonas pusilla</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Worden, A. Z. et al. Green evolution and dynamic adaptations revealed by genomes of the marine picoeukaryotes Micromonas. Science 324, 268–272 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d42345e1996">6</a></sup>, <i>Micromonas commoda</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Worden, A. Z. et al. Green evolution and dynamic adaptations revealed by genomes of the marine picoeukaryotes Micromonas. Science 324, 268–272 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d42345e2002">6</a></sup>, <i>Ostreococcus tauri</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Derelle, E. et al. Genome analysis of the smallest free-living eukaryote Ostreococcus tauri unveils many unique features. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 103, 11647–11652 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d42345e2008">4</a></sup>, <i>Ostreococcus lucimarinus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Palenik, B. et al. The tiny eukaryote Ostreococcus provides genomic insights into the paradox of plankton speciation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 104, 7705–7710 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d42345e2015">7</a></sup>, and <i>Volvox carteri</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Prochnik, S. E. et al. Genomic analysis of organismal complexity in the multicellular green alga Volvox carteri. Science 329, 223–226 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d42345e2021">10</a></sup> were queried against the <i>Chloropicon</i> chromosomes using TBLASTN searches. Results from gene predictions, RNA mapping and homology searches were loaded in a local Web-Apollo 2.0.2-RC3 server using built-in and custom Perl scripts, and the annotations curated using the sum of all information and Web-Apollo built-in tools.</p><p>Curated GFF3 annotations were exported using Web-Apollo built-in tools, converted to EMBL format with WebApolloGFF3toEMBL.pl and further curated manually using Artemis 16.0.0, wherein locus tags were automatically added using the feature Edit &gt; Automatically Create Gene Names. Amino acid sequences from predicted proteins were exported with EMBLtoPROT.pl and putative functions were inferred independently using InterProScan 5 and BLASTP homology searches against the UniProt Swiss-Prot/TrEMBL databases and the <i>Chlamydomonas</i> and <i>Coccomyxa</i> protein datasets. Curated EMBL annotations were converted to TBL format using the curated product list and EMBLtoTBL.pl. Final accessions were generated and validated with TBL2ASN.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Gene expression analysis</h3><p>RNAseq Illumina reads were mapped against the <i>Chloropicon</i> genome with PASS 2.23 using the parameters described in the previous section. The resulting alignments in SAM format were converted to BAM format, then sorted using Samtools 1.3.1. RNAseq sequencing depths for individual nucleotide positions were inferred with the depth -aa command line option of Samtools. Gene expression levels were inferred by averaging the sequencing depths of all nucleotides from the exons of the corresponding genes in the <i>Chloropicon</i> genome annotation with genes_expressed.pl.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">BUSCO analyses</h3><p>BUSCO 3 was used to compare the quality and completeness of the DNA assemblies and gene annotations of <i>Chloropicon</i> with those of 12 other green algae. The annotated proteins of these algae were queried against the OrthoDB v10 database, which contains the 2168 near-universal single-copy orthologs found in the Chlorophyta.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Variant identification and GC-content analyses</h3><p>Variants were identified by mapping genomic reads onto the chromosome assemblies that were masked for repeated sequences using RepeatModeler 1.0.11 and RepeatMasker 4.0.7 with the -no_IS and -nolow options. The Illumina paired-end reads were aligned using minimap2 using the -x sr preset and both SNPs and small indels were called with VarScan2 2.4.3 using a minimum variant frequency (–min-var-freq) of 0.2 and the minimum number of supporting reads (–min-reads2) set to 50, as implemented in get_SNP.pl 1.9f from the SSRG pipeline (<a href="https://github.com/PombertLab">https://github.com/PombertLab</a>). For all SNP positions, the nucleotides corresponding to the observed alleles together with their frequencies were extracted from the varscan2 VCF files with sort_SNPs.pl, and plots showing the distribution of SNP allelic frequencies on one or more chromosomes were generated with Rstudio 1.1.453 and R 3.5.0 using the fitdistrplus 1.0–9 package. Large indels were identified with the PacBio structural variant calling and analysis tools (PBSV 2.1.1). Chromosomal GC contents were plotted with Circos using 1000-nt window and 500-nt slide parameters with GC_content_to_Circos.pl.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Determination of sequencing depth</h3><p>Illumina reads (paired-ends, mate pairs, paired-ends + mate pairs) and PacBio subreads were aligned independently on the <i>Chloropicon</i> unmasked nuclear genome using minimap2. Sequencing depth of each chromosome was determined from the BAM alignment files with the Samtools depth function as implemented in get_SNPs.pl. The same methodology was used to calculate the PacBio sequencing depth of the chloroplast and mitochondrial genomes (GenBank accession KJ746601 and MK085998, respectively). Sequencing depths were plotted with Circos using 10,000-nt window and 5000-nt slide parameters with Coverage_to_Circos.pl.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Comparative analyses of gene content and gene order</h3><p>Putative orthologs of the <i>Chloropicon</i> gene products in the 13 green algae mentioned in the section Genome annotations were searched by homology searches using BLASTP (<i>E</i>-value cutoff = 1.0E-10) and TBLASTN (<i>E</i>-value cutoff = 1.0E-10). BLASTP and TBLASTN hits were parsed at the desired cutoff using shared_proteins.pl. Maps of metabolic pathway were retrieved from the KEGG online repository<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Kanehisa, M., Sato, Y., Furumichi, M., Morishima, K. &amp; Tanabe, M. New approach for understanding genome variations in KEGG. Nucleic Acids Res. 47, D590–D595 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d42345e2106">78</a></sup> with KEGG.sh, and KEGG orthologs (KOs) were assigned for each algal dataset with GhostKOALA. The identified KOs were concatenated into distinct KEGG pathway matrices with KOs_to_matrices.pl and plotted using the Bioconductor ComplexHeatmap package as implemented in R 3.5.0 with MatrixR_plotter.pl.</p><p>Gene order data were extracted from the GFF annotation files of the compared algal genomes with gff_to_synteny.pl using regular expressions tailored to account for the variations between GFF files. Orthologous proteins encoded by shared gene pairs were identified by BLASTP searches with get_synteny.pl, iteratively allowing for 0, 1, 5, 10, and 50 genes interspersed between potential pairs. In this analysis, the genes in each potential shared pair had to be in the same relative orientation in the compared genomes, present on a single contig/chromosome in each genome (highly fragmented genomes will inherently produce fewer gene pairs with this assumption), and distant by no more than the number of genes allowed in-between (genes were allowed in-between to account for indels and for inaccurate/spurious predictions resulting in low homology or extraneously predicted products). Gene colocalization on similar chromosomes or contigs between genomes was investigated with chromosome_explorer.pl and the output was converted to matrices with get_matrix.pl. Gene colocalization data were plotted using the Bioconductor ComplexHeatmap package as implemented in R 3.4.0 with script contatenate_matrices.pl.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Phylogenetic analyses</h3><p>To determine the phylogenetic relationships of <i>Chloropicon</i> with the 12 algae selected for comparative genome analyses, a maximum likelihood tree was inferred from the proteins encoded by the orthologous single-copy genes present in all species. These genes were identified using Orthofinder 2.3.1; individual protein products were aligned with MAFFT 7.407 using the L-INS-i method; unambiguously aligned regions of each alignment were removed using BMGE 1.12 and default parameters; and finally a supermatrix was created with create_supermatrix.pl. The resulting dataset was analyzed using IQ-TREE 1.6.7 and the GTR20 + R4 model. Separate phylogenies were also inferred from the <i>Chloropicon</i> PrpB, PrpC, PrpD and PrpF proteins and their orthologs from the NCBI nr database. Protein alignments were carried out with MAFFT 7.313 and the–auto option, and after filtration of each alignment using TrimAl 1.3 and the block = 6, gt = 0.7, st = 0.005 and sw = 3 options, trees were inferred with IQ-TREE 1.6.1 using the automatically selected best-fit model. For all trees, confidence of branch points was estimated by ultrafast bootstrap analysis with 1000 replicates<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Hoang, D. T., Chernomor, O., von Haeseler, A., Minh, B. Q. &amp; Vinh, L. S. UFBoot2: Improving the ultrafast bootstrap approximation. Mol. Biol. Evol. 35, 518–522 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d42345e2127">79</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Reporting summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#MOESM2">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>Data supporting the findings of this work are available within the paper and its Supplementary Information files. A reporting summary for this Article is available as a Supplementary Information file. The datasets generated and analyzed during the current study are available from the corresponding author upon request. The annotated <i>Chloropicon</i> genome sequence has been deposited in the NCBI Genbank database (accessions <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/CP031034.1/">CP031034</a>-<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/CP031053">CP031053</a>). All raw DNA sequencing (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/SRR8185492">SRR8185492</a>-<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRR8185497">SRR8185497</a>) and RNAseq (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRR8992761">SRR8992761</a>) data have been submitted to the NCBI Sequence Read Archive. All these data are accessible under the NCBI Bioproject PRJNA316521 [<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/PRJNA316521">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/PRJNA316521</a>]. The source data underlying Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#Fig1">1</a>d, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#Fig4">4</a>a–d and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12014-x#Fig5">5a–c</a> are provided as a Source Data file.</p>

                </p></li><li class="c-article-references__item js-c-reading-companion-references-item" itemprop="citation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle"><meta itemprop="author" content="SE.                    href="https://doi.org/10.3390/catal10091072"
                   id="title-link-4">
                  Phylogeny and Structure of Fatty Acid Photodecarboxylases and Glucose-Methanol-Choline Oxidoreductases
                </a>
              </h3>

                    class="c-article-further-reading__authors-list js-list-authors-3 js-etal-collapsed" data-component-authors-activator="authors-list"
                    id="authors-4">
                  <li><span
                        class="js-separator"></span><span>Vladimir A. Aleksenko</span></li><li><span
